Jamison Brereton

18 (314)
Indra
V─Бmadeva Gautama
13 verses: triс╣гс╣нubh
A famous and enigmatic hymn recounting the prodigious and unnatural birth of Indra, partly in dialogue form. The hymn begins with a two-verse exchange between IndraтАЩs mother and the as-yet-unborn Indra: she tries to persuade him to be born in normal fashion, but, like many heroes world-wide, he insists on an unusual exit, from her side. Indra is already conscious of his great destiny (see vs. 2cd). The preg
nancy has lasted abnormally long (see vs. 4ab), and after the birth IndraтАЩs mother appears to abandon him to make his own way (vss. 3ab, 4ab, 8ab, 10cd), but also hides him (vs. 5ab) and worries about his fate (vs. 11ab). Again like heroes the world over, Indra is born full-grown and ready to perform great feats (see vs. 5cd), so that his motherтАЩs protection is not necessary. In verse 3 he follow his mother as she abandons him and ends up at Tvaс╣гс╣нarтАЩs house, where he drinks Tvaс╣гс╣нarтАЩs soma. Elsewhere Tvaс╣гс╣нar is presented as IndraтАЩs father (e.g., III.48.2тАУ4, possibly in the previous hymn IV.17.4), and by stealing and drinking his soma, Indra defeats him (see III.48, where IndraтАЩs mother abets him). Our verse contains no mention of this rivalry and conflict, but it may be alluded to indirectly toward the end of the hymn (see below).
In the middle part of the hymn (vss. 6тАУ9) another set of voices (at least in our opinion) joins the dialogue, that of various waters. These waters seem to represent benevolent foster mothers (see esp. vss. 7тАУ8), like the rivers attending the birth of Urva┼Ы─лтАЩs son in X.95.7, and their protective aid for Indra contrasts with his motherтАЩs ambivalence and alienation. But they are also assimilated to the waters released by Indra in the Vr╠еtra myth, as IndraтАЩs mother makes explicit in verse 7. It may be that both identities were suggested by a natural phenomenon, the breaking of a preg
nant womanтАЩs waters right before birth; the sudden release of those waters is remi niscent of the release in the Vr╠еtra myth, while the association of the waters with the infant being born suggests their beneficent relationship with the fetus in the womb. IndraтАЩs mother ends this section by disavowing any blame for IndraтАЩs temporary disadvantage in the IndraтАУVr╠еtra battle (vs. 9). If we are correct, the second p─Бda of this verse alludes glancingly to snakesтАЩ ability to reconfigure their jaws to swallow large prey, a striking naturalistic imageтАФone of the incidental fragments of realia to be gleaned from the R╠еgveda.
The final verses of the hymn are even more puzzling than what precedes them. After the episode of the waters and the Vr╠еtra battle, we return to the original scene of IndraтАЩs birth and his motherтАЩs abandonment of him (vs. 10), though in her worry about his isolation she follows him (vs. 11ab), a mirror image of him following her in verse 3ab. He does find a companion to aid in the Vr╠еtra battle, namely Viс╣гс╣Зu, in verse 11cd. The next verse (12) asks a series of rhetorical questionsтАФor they would be rhetorical if we were certain of the answers. The questions center around a family tragedy of a type very familiar from world mythology: a father with mur derous intent toward his son (possibly still in the womb; vs. 12b) and the killing of the father by the son (vs. 12d), an act that leaves his mother a widow (vs. 12a). Is the father Tvaс╣гс╣нar, mentioned briefly and without hostility in the earlier part of the hymn (vs. 3), or Vr╠еtra, whose destruction was depicted several times (vss. 7cd, 9)тАФor both, or neither? In any case the questions are reminiscent of the question near the end of the famous Indra-Vr╠еtra hymn I.32, when Indra flees after winning the battle: verse 14 тАЬWhom did you see, Indra, as the avenger of the serpent when fear came into your heart after you smashed him, / and when you crossed over the ninety-nine flowing rivers, like a frightened falcon through the airy realms?тАЭ And the final verse of IV.18 (13) seems to depict Indra after such a flight, alone and in exile, eating taboo food out of necessity and witness to the dishonoring of his mother because of her widowed state. His own condition only improves when the falcon steals soma and brings it to him (vs. 13d), a tale that will be treated later in this cycle in IV.26тАУ27.
Although the abrupt shifts of scene and chronology and the lack of context in each new scene produce some frustration in the reader (not to speak of the

translator), the vivid focus of each episode and the dramatic tension in each snippet of speech cause the hymn to resonate in the imagination long after a more rational account would have done.

English translation:

тАЬIndra speaks: This is the old and recognized path by which all the gods are born; so, when full-grown, let him be born in the same manner; let him not cause the loss of this his mother.тАЭ

[IndraтАЩs Mother:] Here is the ancient proven path from which all the gods were born.
Just from it should he be born full-grown.тАФDo not cause your mother to fall [=miscarry] in that way.

THIS is the ancient and accepted pathway by which all Gods have come into existence.
Hereby could one be born though waxen mighty. Let him not, otherwise, destroy his Mother.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬV─Бmadeva speaks: Let me not come forth by this path, for it is difficult (of issue); let me come forth obliquely from the side; many acts unperformed by others are to be accomplished by me; let me contend (in war) with one (enemy), in controversy with one opponent.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

[Indra:] I will not go out from thereтАФitтАЩs a hard plunge. I will go out crossways, from your side.
Many are the things as yet undone that are to be done by me: I will do battle with one and make peace with another.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The verb ├бv─Бsс╣Ыjanta lacks an overt object. This may be because it is middle, in contrast to the generally transitive active to this stem; so most tr., incl. the published translation

(тАЬlet goтАЭ). However, the -anta may be an -anta replacement of the usual type (see my 1979 IIJ article), and the verb form should be taken as a transitive equivalent to the active, with unexpressed obj. Indra. (This is how Kulikov [-ya-pres., p. 289] takes it, flg. a suggestion of LubotskyтАЩs – though -anta replacement is not mentioned: тАЬThe gods abandoned [Indra], like the feeble ones.тАЭ) I am of two minds. The situation depicted is presumably the gods finking out on Indra when the Vс╣Ыtra battle looms; this might suggest that we should supply Indra as object: English тАЬlet Indra downтАЭ would be an almost exact match. But the simile j├нvrayo n├б тАЬlike old/feeble (men)тАЭ does not fit this scenario as well; it implies that their powers simply failed them.

They тАЬlet goтАЭ – the stuffing just went out of them, as it were.

The usual problem with bh├║vaс╕е – injunctive (so apparently Geldner, also the published

tr.) or subjunctive (so apparently Witzel Got┼Н). I assume that this verb refers to what happened after the event of p─Бda a: with the gods out of contention, Indra comes into his own as the universal monarch (samr─Б╠Бj-) and takes his true and proper place (saty├бyoni-). The use of -yoni- here is reminiscent of the passage in a nearby Indra hymn, IV.16.10, where Indra is urged to sit down on his own y├│ni- (sv├й y├│nau) so that he can be recognized.

Griffith

Not this way go I forth: hard is the passage. Forth from the side obliquely will I issue.
Much that is yet undone must I accomplish; one must I combat and the other question.

Geldner

Ich mag nicht hier hinausgehen; dies ist ein ├╝bler Durchgang. Ich will quer zur Seite hinausgehen. Vieles noch nie getane mu├Я ich tun; mit dem einen will ich k├дmpfen, mit dem anderen mich verst├дndigen.

Grassmann

┬╗Nicht geh auf dem ich, da ist schlechter Durchgang, quer von der Seite will hervor ich gehen; Noch ungethanes muss ich viel vollbringen, mit jenem k├дmpfen und mit diesem reden,┬л

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬHe, (Indra), has asserted (that it will) cause the death of my mother; let me not proceed by the usual way, but proceed quickly, according (to my will); in the dwelling of Tvaс╣гс╣н─Б Indra drank the costly Soma from the vessels of the offerers.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

V─Бmadeva vindicates his own wilfulness by the example of Indra, who came to Tvaс╣гс╣н─Б’s house uninvited, and, by force, drank the Soma prepared for other gods; tvaс╣гс╣н─Б havaputro v─лndram somam─Бharat (Taittir─лya Saс╣Гhit─Б 2.4.12)

Jamison Brereton

[Narrator:] He gazed after his mother going away. тАЬI cannot not followтАФ now shall I follow!тАЭ
In the house of Tvaс╣гс╣нar, Indra drank soma, a hundredтАЩs worth of the pressed (drink) in the two cups.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The phrase abudhy├бm ├бbudhyam─Бnaс╣Г suс╣гup─Бс╣З├бm тАЬnot to be awakened, unawakening, gone to sleepтАЭ must be proleptic, expressing the state the serpent will be in after Indra has done his work on him: тАШput to sleepтАЩ and similar idioms are standard euphemisms for death in Vedic, as in many languages (e.g., modern English). See my тАЬтАШSleepтАЩ in Vedic and Indo-European,тАЭ Zeitschrift f├╝r vergl.

Sprachforschung (KZ) 96 (1982/83) 6-16. I do not think, pace most tr., that this depicts a drowsy Vс╣Ыtra whom Indra woke up to fight. For further disc. see I.103.7 and comm. thereon.

The hapax aparv├бn at the end of d is picked up by p├бrvat─Бn─Бm at the end of 4d. (They are, of course, synchronically unrelated.)

Griffith

He bent his eye upon the dying Mother: My word I now withdraw. That way I follow.
In Tvastar’s dwelling India drank the Soma, a hundredworth of juice pressed from the mortar.

Geldner

Er blickte der fortgehenden Mutter nach: “Ich will nicht zur├╝ckbleiben, ich will doch nachgehen.” Im Hause des Tvastri trank Indra den Soma, der hundert K├╝he wert war, von dem in den Gef├д├Яen gepre├Яten.

Grassmann

Er blickte nach der Mutter, da sie hinschied, ┬╗Ich nehm’ zur├╝ck das Wort, dort will ich gehn nun┬л, In Tvaschtar’s Hause trank dann Indra Soma, den goldeswerthen, der die Schalen f├╝llte.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬAditi speaks: What irregular act has he committed whom (I, his mother), bore for a thousand months and for many years? there is no analogy between him and those who have been or will be born.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Aditi defends her son upon the plural a that, as his period of gestation was marvellous, his actions are not to be compared with those of any others

Jamison Brereton

[Narrator:] How could she put aside the one whom she bore for a thousand months and many autumns?
For surely he has no equal among those born and those yet to be born.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted above ad IV.16.14 this group of Indra hymns contains a set of anomalously built medial participles to the roots тИЪva┼Ы тАШdesireтАЩ and тИЪvas тАШwearтАЩ. Here medial thematic u┼Ы├бm─Бna- is doubly unexpected: this root builds a root pres., with a weak grade u┼Ы, but it is only active (with an extremely well-attested act. part. u┼Ы├бnt-), save for three occurrences of athem. u┼Ы─Бn├б-. And there is no other trace of a 6th class thematic present to account for the -m─Бna-suffix. Neither of these anomalies seems to me particularly serious or hard to account for. As for the middle voice, verbs of desiring seem to fall naturally into the semantic realm of the middle voice, so that a transfer of the participle would not be surprising. Moreover, if we take the redupl.

part. v─Бva┼Ы─Бn├б- as belonging to a pf. of this root (contra K├╝mmel, who assigns all these forms to тИЪv─Б┼Ы тАШbellowтАЩ), there is a parallel formation with the same voice and same meaning. As for the thematic suffix, Narten (MSS 16: 82 = KlSch 128) suggests that this poet has a penchant for -m─Бna-; if this explanation seems insufficient (and it does to me – what about u┼Ы─Бn├б- in IV.23.1 as well as numerous well-behaved athem.

middle participles in his oeuvre) – one might point to the ambiguous 3rd pl. act.

u┼Ы├бnti (3x), which is presumably the 3rd pl. of the root pres., but could belong also to a 6th class present. (However, I note that the three 3rd pl. forms are found only in I and X.) I take ├│jaс╕е as an acc. of respect with the part.

Geldner sees p─Бda d as reflecting the Winged Mountains story, but this doesnтАЩt seem evident to me.

Griffith

What strange act shall he do, he whom his Mother bore for a thousand months and many autumns?
No peer hath he among those born already, nor among those who shall be born hereafter.

Geldner

Warum will sie den beiseite schaffen, den sie tausend Monate und viele Herbste getragen hat? Denn es gibt ja nicht seinesgleichen unter den Geborenen und unter den K├╝nftigen.

Grassmann

Was wird vor allem der thun, den die Mutter wol tausend Monde trug und viele Jahre? Denn nichts ist diesem einen zu vergleichen von allem, was geboren ist und sein wird.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬDeeming it disreputable (that he should be brought forth) in secret, his mother endowed (Indra) with (extraordinary) vigour; therefore, as soon as born he sprung up of his own accord, invested with splendour, and filled both heaven and earth.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

In secret: in the privacy of the lying-in chamber, unworthy of so great a divinity

Jamison Brereton

[Narrator:] Thinking him somehow a disgrace, his mother concealed Indra, who overflowed with heroic strength.
But he stood up on his own, clothing himself in a cloak. He filled the two world-halves as he was being born.

Jamison Brereton Notes

P─Бda a presents some interpretational difficulties that I think can be resolved by considering it an example of disharmony in a simile (see my 1982 IIJ article). I take the verb abh├н pr├б dadruс╕е as belonging to тИЪdс╣Э тАШsplit, burstтАЩ (see below for another possibility). In the simile j├бnayo n├б g├бrbham it has transitive value, with the object expressing the contents that has been burst out (not the container), hence тАЬas women (burst out) their embryo.тАЭ In the frame I take the mountains that ended the previous p─Бda (4d) as the subject and the verb as intransitive: тАЬthey burst.тАЭ (This is also GeldnerтАЩs and Witzel Got┼НтАЩs interpr., as well as K├╝mmelтАЩs [230].) Oldenberg suggests as another alternative that the verb can be transitive, with mountains as subject and rivers as object, but I would prefer to supply as little as possible. Oldenberg suggests yet another possibility, that the verb actually belongs to the root тИЪdr─Б тАШrunтАЩ. Although this does not make sense for the simile (as Oldenberg notes), it could work for the frame – though in that case тАШriversтАЩ might be a better subject. In that case we would have a pun separating the simile and frame (тАЬ[the rivers] ran [тИЪdr─Б], as women burst out [тИЪdс╣Э] their embryoтАЭ), rather than a mismatch of usages of a single lexical item. I prefer the single-root solution.

The 2nd p─Бda also has a somewhat skewed expression. In this context we would expect the entities that тАЬwent/drove forth all at onceтАЭ to be the released waters, who are certainly the topic of the 2nd hemistich. But instead it is тАШstonesтАЩ (├бdrayaс╕е).

Now this is probably, on the one hand, a particularly vivid image of the mountains suddenly bursting and sending forth an explosion of stones, a rockslide. But on the other hand, p─Бda-final ├бdrayaс╕е produces a Jagat─л cadence in a hymn that is otherwise entirely Triс╣гс╣нubh. Oldenberg suggests (without great enthusiasm, as far as I can see) an emendation to abl. *├бdreс╕е тАШfrom the stoneтАЩ, which would fix both the meter and the image. I wonder if ├бdrayaс╕е is a poetic trick: we expect the subject *─Б╠Бpaс╕е тАШwatersтАЩ – which would provide both the standard Vс╣Ыtra-myth denouement and a good Triс╣гс╣нubh cadence – and instead get a twist of both sense and meter.

Griffith

Deeming him a reproach, his mother hid him, Indra, endowed with all heroic valour.
Then up he sprang himself, assumed his vesture, and filled, as soon as born, the earth and heaven.

Geldner

Als ob sie ihn f├╝r einen Schandfleck hielte, hat die Mutter den Indra versteckt, der von Manneskraft strotzte. Da erhob er sich, selbst sein Gewand umlegend; der Neugeborene f├╝llte beide Welth├дlften an.

Grassmann

Ihn f├╝r gering erachtend barg die Mutter, den Indra, den von Heldenkraft erf├╝llten, Da brach er vor, in eignes Kleid sich h├╝llend, erf├╝llte beide Welten, der geborne.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬThese (rivers) flew murmuring as if, being filled with water, they were uttering sounds (of joy); ask them what is this they say; what is the encompassing cloud that the waters break through?тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Ask them what is this they say: i.e., they are proclaiming the greatness of Indra, by which, and not by their own efforts, they have been extricated from the cloud

Jamison Brereton

[Indra:] These (waters) flow, babbling, like truthful women together shouting their witness.
Ask them! What is this they are saying? What rock, what barrier are the waters battering?

Jamison Brereton Notes

As suggested in the published introduction, the (real) waters in the amniotic sac that тАЬbreakтАЭ right before birth and the (mythological) waters confined by Vс╣Ыtra and released by Indra are conflated here. Indra may be speaking from within the womb about the waters there battering the womb itself for release, though the waters in the Vс╣Ыtra myth would not be far from the audienceтАЩs mind. If Indra the fetus is immersed in these amniotic fluids, their sloshing sounds would surround him – and it would be appropriate to ask his mother what they are saying.

The simile in b, с╣Ыt─Б╠Бvar─лr iva saс╣Гkr├│┼Ыam─Бс╣З─Бс╕е тАЬlike truthful women together shouting their witnessтАЭ may have a quasi-legal resonance. The root тИЪkru┼Ы is later used for raising a hue and cry on witnessing a crime (vel sim.), such as a R─Бkс╣гasa abduction (see my Sacr.Wife 233). Configuring the waters as truthful and articulate witnesses in this p─Бda leads directly to the suggestion in the next p─Бda that they should be asked what they are saying.

06-09 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

For my interpr. of the speakers in these vss. and the role of the waters in the myth, see published introduction. Most tr. take the vss. as all spoken by IndraтАЩs mother (Geldner [/Witzel Got┼Н], Doniger), whereas I distribute them to a variety of voices: 6 Indra, 7 IndraтАЩs mother, 8 waters, 9 IndraтАЩs mother. As I see it, in 6 Indra prompts his mother to ask the waters questions; in 7 she rather sarcastically and belittlingly asks questions about them, whom she seems to accuse of trying to lay claim to her son. They respond directly to him in 8, reminding him of his motherтАЩs dereliction of maternal duty and suggesting that they are better at mothering him than she is. So that she rather defiantly points out in 9 that subsequent negative things that happened to him were not her fault.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The responsive phrases k├нm тАж bhananti (6c) and k├нm тАж bhananta (7a) provide a textbook case of -anta replacement. See my 1979 IIJ 21 article, pace Got┼НтАЩs (1st Kl., 222) characterization of bhananta as тАЬreziprok.тАЭ

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬWhat do the sacred expiatory strains declare to me? the waters reeive the reproach of Indra; my son has slain Vс╣Ыtra with the mighty thunderbolt; he has set those rivers free.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

What do the sacred strains declare: kinuс╣гvid asmai nivido manante: the nivids are certain verses repeated at some sacrifices to Indra and the Maruts in their honour, and are tantamount to an acquittal of the charge imputed to Indra, and here anticipated by Aditi of brhmanicide, Vс╣Ыtra being a brahman; the crime was transferred to the waters in the shape of foam

Jamison Brereton

[IndraтАЩs Mother:] What did they say as invitations to him? Do the waters intend to take on IndraтАЩs disgrace?
It was my son who set loose these rivers, after having smashed Vr╠еtra with his great murderous weapon.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This vs. celebrates the fructifying liquid that Indra released by destroying Vс╣Ыtra and depicts its effects on humans (specifically females)(ab), the landscape (c), and livestock (d). The first hemistich is a cleverly constructed echo chamber, because the females being made to swell (that is, get pregnant) there probably stand for the waters, but are also compared to waters. In other words the waters are being compared to waters, by way of the intermediate тАШunwed girlsтАЩ (agr├║vaс╕е). This is also something of a dig at Vс╣Ыtra, who hadnтАЩt managed to make them pregnant though he is sometimes called their husband (cf., e.g., d─Бs├б-patn─л- тАШhaving a D─Бsa as husbandтАЩ in I.32.11, etc.). IndraтАЩs role as their real husband is embodied in the final word of the vs. d├бс╣Гsupatn─лс╕е (however we interpret the rest of it; see below).

In the simile nabhanv├▓ n├б v├бkv─Б(с╕е), v├бkva- belongs to the root тИЪva├▒c тАШsurge, undulate, billowтАЩ. The stem nabhan├║- is found only here and in V.59.7 and is transparently a derivative of the root тИЪnabh тАШburst, explodeтАЩ. Oldenberg suggests the verbal meaning тАШsich spaltenтАЩ with nominal тАШSpaltтАЩ(тАШsplit, cleftтАЩ). However, in both passages I think the nominal form refers not to the aftermath of the verbal action but rather to the process – the spurts sent forth by the explosion (rather like the stones in 5b). The image is visually arresting (at least to me).

The sense of dhvasr├б- in b also requires some discussion. The root тИЪdhvaс╣Гs is variously glossed (e.g., EWA s.v. тАШzerstieben, zerst├дuben, zerbr├╢cklenтАЩ), but in my view the тАШspray, scatterтАЩ sense is far less prominent than тАШoccludeтАЩ (with smoke, dust, or other concealing substance), a sense also found in derivatives like dhvasm├бn- тАШmiasma, (clouds of) smokeтАЩ. Thus to my mind the adj. dvasr├б- means in the first instance тАШoccluded, dustyтАЩ; I have pushed this slightly to тАШparchedтАЩ, from something like тАШdry as dustтАЩ. GeldnerтАЩs тАЬdie dahinschwindendenтАЭ (dwindling away) conveys something of the same sense of weakness and lack of fertility, but I donтАЩt know how he arrived at it.

с╣Ыtaj├▒─Б╠Бс╕е is identified as a nom. sg. m. modifying Indra by Grassmann, so also Scarlatta (177). It can just as easily be an acc. pl. fem. modifying the young women / waters, as Geldner, Witzel Got┼Н, and the published translation take it. Given that the waters in the adjacent hymn, IV.18.6, are called с╣Ыt─Б╠Бvar─лс╕е, the latter analysis seems preferable – although it might be even better to read it with both referents.

The published translation analyzes d├бс╣Гsupatn─л- as having a first member d├бс╣Гsu-, an adjective тАШwondrousтАЩ related to d├бс╣Гsas- тАШwondrous powerтАЩ (so Grassmann). However, the prevailing interpr. is that it is either a cmpd d├бс╣Г-supatn─л- or a two-word sequence d├бс╣Г *sup├бtn─лс╕е, with, in either case, a form of d├бm- тАШhouseтАЩ (cf. d├бс╣Гpati-, p├бtir d├бn).

The complex is then to be rendered тАШhaving a good husband in the houseтАЩ vel sim.

Alternatively Geldner (n. 7d) suggests that it might be a metathesis of *su-daс╣Гpatn─лс╕е (given without accent), which seems quite unlikely. Although I think the form plays off d├бmpati-, I am still inclined towards the тАШwondrousтАЩ interpr., because of the deeds that have just been ascribed to Indra.

06-09 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

For my interpr. of the speakers in these vss. and the role of the waters in the myth, see published introduction. Most tr. take the vss. as all spoken by IndraтАЩs mother (Geldner [/Witzel Got┼Н], Doniger), whereas I distribute them to a variety of voices: 6 Indra, 7 IndraтАЩs mother, 8 waters, 9 IndraтАЩs mother. As I see it, in 6 Indra prompts his mother to ask the waters questions; in 7 she rather sarcastically and belittlingly asks questions about them, whom she seems to accuse of trying to lay claim to her son. They respond directly to him in 8, reminding him of his motherтАЩs dereliction of maternal duty and suggesting that they are better at mothering him than she is. So that she rather defiantly points out in 9 that subsequent negative things that happened to him were not her fault.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬV─Бmadeva speaks: exulting one youthful mother brought you forth; exulting, Kuс╣гav─Б swallowed you; exulting, the waters gave delight to the infant; Indra, exulting, rose up by his strength.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Kuс╣гav─Б = a r─Бkс╣гasi, whom Indra, although at first swalled by her, drove out of the lying-in chamber

Jamison Brereton

[Various voices of the waters:] It was not because of me that the young woman cast you aside. It was not because of me that Kuс╣гav─Б (Evil
Birth) swallowed you.
But it was certainly because of me that the waters would show mercy to the child. It was certainly because of me that Indra stood up with his might.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The question in this vs. is what to do with g┼лrt─Б╠Б(с╕е). The standard tr. take it as modifying the temporal expression p┼лrv─л╠Бr uс╣г├бsaс╕е ┼Ыar├бda┼Ы ca – hence, e.g., GeldnerтАЩs тАЬ[v]iele gelobte Morgen und Herbste.тАЭ This is grammatically fine and perhaps also supported by the fact that the adj. is in the same p─Бda as the temporal expression.

Still, I am somewhat unsatisfied by this interpr. On the one hand, as Klein points out (DGRV I.74), this small group of V─Бmadeva Indra hymns contains three similar temporal expressions (IV.16.19, 18.4, and here), and the only adjectives are quantitative ones, so тАШwelcomed, besung, praisedтАЩ would be an intrusion in the formulaic language. Moreover, sv├бg┼лrta- тАШself-greeted, i.e., gurglingтАЩ is used twice of rivers (I.140.13 s├нndhavaс╕е, X.95.7 nady├ас╕е), and something like that would fit semantically here. The problem of course is that s├нndhu- is masc., and so g┼лrt─Б╠Бс╕е cannot modify acc. pl. s├нndh┼лn as the published translation implies. It is possible that the expression s├нndhava┼Ы ca sv├бg┼лrt─Бс╕е in I.140.13 was transposed to our passage without adjusting the gender. More likely is that the acc. pl. of another, feminne word for rivers, streams, or waters should be supplied: nady├ас╕е- as in X.95.7, s─лr─Б╠Бс╕е as in p─Бda c of this vs., or ap├бс╕е, the default watery referent in the Vс╣Ыtra myth. The tr. should be emended to better reflect this: тАЬтАж he set loose the welcomed [/gurgling]

*(waters/streams and) the rivers.тАЭ Strikingly sv├бg┼лrta-, which occurs only 4x total in the RV, appears two vss. later (10c) in the same metrical position with the same sandhi form. It there modifies ├бp─Бс╣Гsi тАШlaborsтАЩ. Is it too fanciful to suggest that that phrase, sv├бg┼лrt─Б, ├бp─Бс╣Гsi, is meant to invoke *(sv├б)g┼лrt─Б, *ap├бс╕е here? For further disc. see also Oldenberg ad loc.

06-09 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

For my interpr. of the speakers in these vss. and the role of the waters in the myth, see published introduction. Most tr. take the vss. as all spoken by IndraтАЩs mother (Geldner [/Witzel Got┼Н], Doniger), whereas I distribute them to a variety of voices: 6 Indra, 7 IndraтАЩs mother, 8 waters, 9 IndraтАЩs mother. As I see it, in 6 Indra prompts his mother to ask the waters questions; in 7 she rather sarcastically and belittlingly asks questions about them, whom she seems to accuse of trying to lay claim to her son. They respond directly to him in 8, reminding him of his motherтАЩs dereliction of maternal duty and suggesting that they are better at mothering him than she is. So that she rather defiantly points out in 9 that subsequent negative things that happened to him were not her fault.

Griffith

I cast thee from me, mine,-thy youthful mother: thee, mine own offspring, Kusava hath swallowed.
To him, mine infant, were the waters gracious. Indra, my Son, rose up in conquering vigour.

Geldner

Um meinetwillen hat dich die junge Frau nicht beseitigt, noch hat dich um meinetwillen die Kusava verschlungen. Um meinetwillen m├╢gen sich die Gew├дsser des Kindes erbarmt haben, um meinetwillen erhob sich pl├╢tzlich der Indra.

Grassmann

Jetzt stiess dich aus die jugendliche Mutter, und jetzt verschlang dich Kuschava, die Str├╢mung, Jetzt waren hold dem jungen Spross die Wasser, und jetzt erhob mit Allgewalt sich Indra.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬVyaс╣Гsa, exulting and striking (hard blows), smote you, Maghavan, upon the jaw; whereupon, being so smitten, you proved the stronger, and did crush the head of the slave with the thunderbolt.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Vyaс╣Гsa: name of a r─Бkс╣гasa who also attempted to destroy the infant Indra

Jamison Brereton

[IndraтАЩs Mother:] It was not because of me that the cobra, having pierced you down, smashed apart his jaws (to swallow) you, o
bounteous one.
Then, (though) pierced down, having gotten the upper hand, you utterly crushed the head of the D─Бsa with your murderous weapon.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As indicated in the published introduction, the contents of this vs. and the reason for its inclusion in this hymn are both deeply obscure, though the occurrence of the rare stem agr┼л╠Б- тАШunwed girlтАЩ in 7a may have prompted the inclusion of the bizarre anecdote in 9ab. As Geldner’s reff. for ab show, the shunned son of a maiden, the blind man, and the lame man are mentioned together in II.13.12, 15.7, I.112.8; also IV.30.16, 19. So, however ill-assorted, this is a set. The unfortunate son of an agr┼л╠Бis also mentioned in nearby IV.30.16, though there he is only shunned, not eaten by ants.

On ukhach├нd- see Scarlatta (131).

Unfortunately I have nothing further to say about the sense of this vs. I have toyed with the possibility that thereтАЩs a ritual reference here, to the taking out of the offering fire from the householderтАЩs fire and its removal to the east. But, though there might be rough correspondence – very rough – between the first and third parts, the middle part with the blind man and the snake doesn’t work at all, as far as I can see.

06-09 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

For my interpr. of the speakers in these vss. and the role of the waters in the myth, see published introduction. Most tr. take the vss. as all spoken by IndraтАЩs mother (Geldner [/Witzel Got┼Н], Doniger), whereas I distribute them to a variety of voices: 6 Indra, 7 IndraтАЩs mother, 8 waters, 9 IndraтАЩs mother. As I see it, in 6 Indra prompts his mother to ask the waters questions; in 7 she rather sarcastically and belittlingly asks questions about them, whom she seems to accuse of trying to lay claim to her son. They respond directly to him in 8, reminding him of his motherтАЩs dereliction of maternal duty and suggesting that they are better at mothering him than she is. So that she rather defiantly points out in 9 that subsequent negative things that happened to him were not her fault.

Griffith

Thou art mine own, O Maghavan, whom Vyamsa struck to the ground and smote thy jaws in pieces.
But, smitten through, the mastery thou wonnest, and with thy bolt the Dasa’s head thou crushedst.

Geldner

Nicht um meinetwillen hat Vyamsa dich, du Gabenreicher, verwundet und dir beide Kinnbacken abgeschlagen. Obwohl verwundet wardst du da seiner Meister; du hast mit der Waffe den Kopf des Dasa zerschmettert.

Grassmann

Jetzt schlug, o M├дcht’ger, dir die beiden Kiefern, Viansa ab, der Unhold, dich verwundend; Verwundet dann errangest doch den Sieg du zerschlugst des D├дmons Haupt mit deiner Keule.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬAs a helper bears a calf, his mother, (Aditi), bore Indra, mature (in years), strong, irresisitble, vigorous, energetic, invincible, (destined) to follow his own course, heedful of his person n.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

[Narrator:] The heifer gave birth to the sturdy, powerfully charging, unassailable bull, the brawny Indra.
The unlicked calfтАФhis mother impelled him to wander, seeking by
himself a way for himself.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Contra the standard tr. and interpr., I take ─Бha as 1st sg. This is the summary vs. of the hymn (with vs. 11 simply the V─Бmadeva Indra refrain), and in such vss. the poet often speaks in his own person or that of the group, referring to the hymn that has just been recited. This vs. entirely fits that pattern. I also interpr. the enclitic te not only as a genitive with the deeds, but also as a dative with the part.

vid├║с╣гe, identifying Indra as the knowing audience. (And who better than Indra to know his own deeds?) ─Бvidv─Б╠Бn is one of the few forms of тИЪvid тАШknowтАЩ cmpded with the preverb ─Б╠Бin the RV. It does not seem to have a clear nuance.

On sv├бg┼лrta- see comm. ad VI.68.4.

Griffith

The Heifer hath brought forth the Strong, the Mighty, the unconquerable Bull, the furious Indra.
The Mother left her unlicked Calf to wander, seeking himself, the path that he would follow.

Geldner

Die Kalbin gebar den st├дmmigen, starken, unangreifbaren Bullen, den kr├дftigen Indra. Die Mutter lie├Я das Kalb ungeleckt laufen, das sich selbst seinen Weg sucht.

Grassmann

Die junge Kuh gebar den starken, raschen, den feisten Stier, den unbezwungnen Indra; Das ungeleckte Kalb entl├дsst zum Gehn sie; das durch sich selbst schon seinen Weg sich suchte.

Elizarenkova

Jamison Brereton Notes

The plot gets a bit murky here. His mother, having sent him off alone in vs. 10, now follows him, with the fear that the gods are abandoning him. This seems to happen much later, just before the Vс╣Ыtra battle and long after the birth and her own abandonment of the baby. But, despite her fears about the other gods, Indra finds a companion on his own – Viс╣гс╣Зu, who is not usually a party to the Vс╣Ыtra battle.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬWho has made your mother a widow? who has sought to slay the sleeping and the walking? what deity has been more gracious than you since you have slain the father, having seized him by the foot?тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Since you have slain the father: yat pr─Бkс╣гiс╣З─Бс╕е pitaram p─Бdagс╣Ыhya; cf. Taittir─лya Saс╣Гhit─Б 6.1.3.6)

Jamison Brereton

Who made your mother a widow? Who tried to smash you as you lay, as you wandered?
What god was merciful toward you when you destroyed your father, having grasped him by the foot? с│У

Jamison Brereton Notes

Jamison Brereton Notes

See the published introduction. for uncertainties about the interpr. of these vss. In some sense they seem to enlarge on the theme of тАЬIndraтАЩs disgraceтАЭ (├нndrasy─Бvady├бm) in 7b – the blood guilt Indra incurs from even sanctioned killing, made far worse by the intra-family slaughter depicted in vs. 12. Which leads to IndraтАЩs extreme loss of status, isolation, and shunning by the other gods in the final vs. ### 12 In light of d, which describes IndraтАЩs killing his father, the question in a, тАЬwho made your mother a widow,тАЭ can only be answered тАЬyou did!тАЭ The final word of the vs. p─Бdagс╣Ы╠Бhya тАШhaving grasped him by the footтАЩ is puzzling. It might seem to exclude Vс╣Ыtra as the victim (and as IndraтАЩs father) since, as a snake, he has no feet – though it might be a way of indicating picking up a snake by its tail. The only other occurrence of this cmpd gerund is in the desperately difficult hymn X.27, vs. 4, where the context is similar and the referent does seem to be Vс╣Ыtra.

Griffith

Who was he then who made thy Mother widow? Who sought to stay thee lying still or moving?

рдЕрдирдпрд╛ рд╡рд╛рдорджреЗрд╡рдГ рд╕реНрд╡рдХреАрдпрдВ рджрд╛рд░рд┐рджреНрд░реНрдпрдВ рд╕реВрдЪрдпрдиреНрдирд┐рджреНрд░рд╕реНрдпреЛрддреНрдХрд░реНрд╖рдорд╛рд╣ ред рд╡рд╛рдорджреЗрд╡реЛрд╜рд╣рдореН рдЕрд╡рд░реНрддреНрдпрд╛ рдЬреАрд╡рдиреЛрдкрд╛рдпрд░рд╛рд╣рд┐рддреНрдпреЗрди рд╢реБрдирдГ рд╢реБрдирдХрд╕рдВрдмрдиреНрдзреАрдирд┐ рдЕрдиреНрддреНрд░рд╛рдгрд┐ рдкреБрд░реАрддрдиреНрддрд┐ рдкреЗрдЪреЗ рдЕрдкрдЪрдореН ред рджреЗрд╡реЗрд╖реБ рджреНрдпреЛрддрдорд╛рдиреЗрд╖реБ рд╡рд╣реНрдпрд╛тАЬрдпрджрд┐рд╖реБ рдордзреНрдпреЗ рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рд╛рджрдиреНрдпрдВ рдорд░реНрдЩрд┐рддрд╛рд░рдВ рд╕реБрдЦрдпрд┐рддрд╛рд░рдВ рди рд╡рд┐рд╡рд┐рджреЗ рди рд▓реЗрднреЗ ред рдХрд┐рдВрдЪ рдЬрд╛рдпрд╛рдВ рд╕реНрд╡рдХреАрдпрд╛рдВ рднрд╛рд░реНрдпрд╛рдореН рдЕрдорд╣реАрдпрдорд╛рдирд╛рдореН рдЕрд╢реНрд▓рд╛рдШрдиреАрдпрд╛рдореН рдЕрдкрд╢реНрдпрдореН рдЕрджреНрд░рд╛рдХреНрд╖рдореН ред рдЕрдз рдЕрдерд╛рдирдиреНрддрд░рдВ рд╢реНрдпреЗрдирдГ рд╢реНрдпреЗрдирд╡рдЪреНрдЫреАрдШреНрд░рдЧрд╛рдореАрдиреНрджреНрд░рдГ рдореЗ рдорд╣реНрдпрдВ рдордзреБ рдордзреБрд░реЛрдкреЗрддрдореБрджрдХрдореН рдЖ рдЬрднрд╛рд░ рдЖрдЬрд╣рд╛рд░ рее рее реирем рее

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬIn extreme destitution I have cooked the entrails of a dog; I have not found a comforter among the gods; I have beheld my wife disrsepected; then the falcon, (Indra), has brought to me sweet water.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

In extreme destitutuin: So Manu has, V─Бmadeva, who well knew right and wrong, was by no means rendered impure, though desirous when oppressed with hunger, of eating the flesh of dogs for the preservation of his life; icchan attum, wishing ot eat; the text has ┼Ыuno ─Бntr─Бс╣Зi pece, I cooked the entrails of a dog; the falcon: i.e., as swift as a hawk, ┼Ыyena vat ┼Ы─лghrag─Бm─лndraс╕е; S┼лkta 18: с╣Ъс╣гi v─Бmadeva, while yet in the womb, was reluctant to be born and chose to come into the world through his mother’s side; aware of his purpose, the mother prayed to Aditi, who thereupon came, with her son Indra, to expostulate with the с╣Ъс╣гi; this is the subject of the S┼лkta

Jamison Brereton

[Indra:] Out of need I cooked for myself the entrails of a dog. I found none who was merciful among the gods.
I saw his wife being dishonored. Then the falcon carried the honey
here to me.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Indra gets the last word in this hymn and, having described his situation in the direst of terms, ends with a note of hope and coming triumph: the falconтАЩs arrival with the soma, to be treated (in even more enigmatic terms) in two nearby hymns, IV.26-27. Since in our hymn the new-born Indra drank soma in Tvaс╣гс╣нarтАЩs house (3c) and presumably had a good dose of it before the Vс╣Ыtra battle, the falconтАЩs stolen soma cannot be the primal soma, though it sometimes mythologically seems to parallel the primal stealing of fire in the Prometheus myth.

A dog-cooker (┼Ыvapaca-) in later texts is a person living outside of societal norms (cf., e.g., MD┼Ъ III.92), grouped with those who have fallen from caste and so forth.

Notice that Indra here finds no one to be merciful to him (n├б тАж vivide marс╕Нit─Б╠Бram), in contrast to the merciful waters when he was a baby (8c).

Most take the dishonored wife to be IndraтАЩs own, but no wife has intruded on the family drama weтАЩve been observing. I assume rather that this is another reference to his mother, who, now that she is a widow, receives slighting treatment.

Jamison Brereton Notes

See the published introduction. for uncertainties about the interpr. of these vss. In some sense they seem to enlarge on the theme of тАЬIndraтАЩs disgraceтАЭ (├нndrasy─Бvady├бm) in 7b – the blood guilt Indra incurs from even sanctioned killing, made far worse by the intra-family slaughter depicted in vs. 12. Which leads to IndraтАЩs extreme loss of status, isolation, and shunning by the other gods in the final vs. ### 12 In light of d, which describes IndraтАЩs killing his father, the question in a, тАЬwho made your mother a widow,тАЭ can only be answered тАЬyou did!тАЭ The final word of the vs. p─Бdagс╣Ы╠Бhya тАШhaving grasped him by the footтАЩ is puzzling. It might seem to exclude Vс╣Ыtra as the victim (and as IndraтАЩs father) since, as a snake, he has no feet – though it might be a way of indicating picking up a snake by its tail. The only other occurrence of this cmpd gerund is in the desperately difficult hymn X.27, vs. 4, where the context is similar and the referent does seem to be Vс╣Ыtra.

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдерд╛рдиреНрд╡рдпрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - (рдЕрд╡рд░реНрддреНрдпрд╛) рдЕрд╡рд░реНрддреНрддрдиреАрдпрд╛рдирд┐ (рд╢реБрдирдГ) рдХреБрдХреНрдХреБрд░рд╕реНрдпреЗрд╡ (рдЖрдиреНрддреНрд░рд╛рдгрд┐) рдЙрджрд░рд╕реНрдерд╛рдГ рд╕реНрдереВрд▓рд╛ рдирд╛рдбреА (рдкреЗрдЪреЗ) рдкрдЪрддрд┐ (рди) (рджреЗрд╡реЗрд╖реБ) рд╡рд┐рджреНрд╡рддреНрд╕реБ (рд╡рд┐рд╡рд┐рджреЗ) рд▓рднрддреЗ (рдорд░реНрдбрд┐рддрд╛рд░рдореН) рд╕реБрдЦрдХрд░рдореН (рдЕрдкрд╢реНрдпрдореН) рдкрд╢реНрдпреЗрдпрдореН (рдЬрд╛рдпрд╛рдореН) рд╕реНрддреНрд░рд┐рдпрдореН (рдЕрдорд╣реАрдпрдорд╛рдирд╛рдореН) рдЕрд╕рддреНрдХреГрддрд╛рдореН (рдЕрдзрд╛) рдирд┐рдкрд╛рддрд╕реНрдп рдЪреЗрддрд┐ рджреАрд░реНрдШрдГред (рдореЗ) рдордо (рд╢реНрдпреЗрдирдГ) рд╢реНрдпреЗрди рдЗрд╡ рд╢реАрдШреНрд░рдЧрдиреНрддрд╛ (рдордзреБ) рдордзреБрд░рдВ рд╡рд┐рдЬреНрдЮрд╛рдирдореН (рдЖ) рд╕рд░реНрд╡рддрдГ (рдЬрднрд╛рд░) рд╣рд░рддрд┐ реерезрейрее

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдерднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - рдЕрддреНрд░ рд╡рд╛рдЪрдХрд▓реБрдкреНрддреЛрдкрдорд╛рд▓рдЩреНрдХрд╛рд░рдГред рд╣реЗ рд░рд╛рдЬрдиреН ! рдпреЗ рдкреБрд░реБрд╖рд╛ рдпрд╛рдГ рд╕реНрддреНрд░рд┐рдпрд╢реНрдЪ рд╡реНрдпрднрд┐рдЪрд╛рд░рдВ рдХреБрд░реНрдпреБрд╕реНрддрд╛рдВрд╕реНрддреАрд╡реНрд░рдВ рджрдгреНрдбрдВ рдиреАрддреНрд╡рд╛ рд╡рд┐рдирд╛рд╢рдп реерезрейрее рдЕрддреНрд░реЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рдореЗрдШрд░рд╛рдЬрд╡рд┐рджреНрд╡рддреНрдХреГрддреНрдпрд╡рд░реНрдгрдирд╛рджреЗрддрджрд░реНрдерд╕реНрдп рдкреВрд░реНрд╡рд╕реВрдХреНрддрд╛рд░реНрдереЗрди рд╕рд╣ рд╕рдЩреНрдЧрддрд┐рд░реНрд╡реЗрджреНрдпрд╛ реерезрейрее рдЗрддрд┐ рд╢реНрд░реАрдорддреНрдкрд░рдорд╣рдВрд╕рдкрд░рд┐рд╡реНрд░рд╛рдЬрдХрд╛рдЪрд╛рд░реНрдпреЗрдг рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрджрд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреАрд╕реНрд╡рд╛рдорд┐рдирд╛ рд╡рд┐рд░рдЪрд┐рддреЗ рд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрддрд╛рд░реНрдпрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рднреНрдпрд╛рдВ рд╡рд┐рднреВрд╖рд┐рддреЗ рд╕реБрдкреНрд░рдорд╛рдгрдпреБрдХреНрдд рдЛрдЧреНрд╡реЗрджрднрд╛рд╖реНрдпреЗ рддреГрддреАрдпрд╛рд╖реНрдЯрдХреЗ рдкрдЮреНрдЪрдореЛрд╜рдзреНрдпрд╛рдпреЛрд╜рд╖реНрдЯрд╛рджрд╢рдВ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрдВ рд╖рдбреНрд╡рд┐рдВрд╢реЛ рд╡рд░реНрдЧрд╢реНрдЪ рд╕рдорд╛рдкреНрддрдГ рее

рд╕рд╡рд┐рддрд╛ рдЬреЛрд╢реА тЖР рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рдо) - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдерднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - рдпрд╛ рдордВрддреНрд░рд╛рдд рд╡рд╛рдЪрдХрд▓реБрдкреНрддреЛрдкрдорд╛рд▓рдВрдХрд╛рд░ рдЖрд╣реЗ. рд╣реЗ рд░рд╛рдЬрд╛ ! рдЬреЗ рдкреБрд░реБрд╖ рд╡ рд╕реНрддреНрд░рд┐рдпрд╛ рд╡реНрдпрднрд┐рдЪрд╛рд░ рдХрд░рддрд╛рдд рддреНрдпрд╛рдВрдирд╛ рдХрдареЛрд░ рджрдВрдб рджреЗрдКрди рддреНрдпрд╛рдВрдЪрд╛ рдирд╛рд╢ рдХрд░рд╛. рее резрей рее
